## Chapter: Study Design

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Study Design in "Mastering Integrated Math 3." In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts and techniques used in designing studies in the field of mathematics. Study design plays a crucial role in ensuring the validity and reliability of research findings, allowing mathematicians to draw meaningful conclusions from their investigations.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into various aspects of study design, including the formulation of research questions, the selection of appropriate study designs, and the implementation of data collection methods. We will also discuss the importance of sampling techniques and the ethical considerations that researchers must take into account when conducting mathematical studies.

One of the key topics we will cover is the concept of randomization. Randomization is a powerful tool that helps eliminate bias and confounding variables, allowing researchers to make causal inferences from their data. We will explore different randomization techniques and their applications in mathematical studies.

Additionally, we will discuss the importance of sample size determination and statistical power analysis. These concepts are essential in ensuring that studies have sufficient statistical power to detect meaningful effects and draw accurate conclusions. We will explore various methods for calculating sample sizes and discuss the factors that influence statistical power.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles and techniques involved in study design in mathematics. You will be equipped with the knowledge and skills necessary to design and conduct rigorous mathematical studies, enabling you to contribute to the advancement of the field.

Let's dive into the world of study design and discover how it shapes the landscape of mathematical research!

### Section: Samples and Surveys

In this section, we will explore the important concepts of samples and surveys in the field of study design. Samples and surveys are crucial tools that researchers use to gather data and make inferences about a larger population. By understanding the different types of samples and survey methods, you will be able to design studies that provide accurate and reliable results.

#### Types of Samples

When conducting a study, it is often impractical or impossible to collect data from an entire population. Instead, researchers select a subset of individuals, known as a sample, to represent the larger population. There are several types of samples that can be used, each with its own advantages and limitations. Let's explore some of the most common types:

1. **Random Sample**: A random sample is a sample in which every individual in the population has an equal chance of being selected. This type of sample helps reduce bias and ensures that the sample is representative of the population. Random samples can be selected using various methods, such as random number generators or random sampling techniques.

2. **Stratified Sample**: In a stratified sample, the population is divided into subgroups, or strata, based on certain characteristics. Then, individuals are randomly selected from each stratum in proportion to their representation in the population. This type of sample ensures that each subgroup is adequately represented in the sample, making it useful when studying populations with distinct characteristics.

3. **Cluster Sample**: A cluster sample involves dividing the population into clusters, such as geographical regions or schools, and randomly selecting a few clusters to include in the sample. All individuals within the selected clusters are then included in the sample. Cluster sampling is often more practical and cost-effective when studying large populations spread across different locations.

4. **Convenience Sample**: A convenience sample is a non-random sample that consists of individuals who are easily accessible or readily available to the researcher. While convenience samples are convenient to obtain, they may not accurately represent the entire population and can introduce bias into the study. Therefore, caution should be exercised when using convenience samples.

5. **Systematic Sample**: In a systematic sample, individuals are selected at regular intervals from a list or sequence. For example, every 10th person on a list may be selected. Systematic sampling can be an efficient way to obtain a representative sample, especially when the population is large and ordered in some way.

It is important to note that the choice of sampling method depends on various factors, such as the research question, available resources, and the characteristics of the population being studied. Researchers must carefully consider these factors to ensure that the sample is representative and the study results can be generalized to the larger population.

In the next subsection, we will explore different survey methods that researchers use to collect data from their samples.

### Section: Samples and Surveys

In this section, we will continue our exploration of samples and surveys in the field of study design. Samples and surveys are essential tools that researchers use to gather data and make inferences about a larger population. By understanding the different types of samples and survey methods, you will be able to design studies that provide accurate and reliable results.

#### Designing Surveys

Designing a survey involves careful planning and consideration of various factors. A well-designed survey ensures that the data collected is relevant, reliable, and representative of the population being studied. Let's explore some key steps in designing surveys:

1. **Define the Objective**: The first step in designing a survey is to clearly define the objective. What specific information are you trying to gather? What research question are you trying to answer? Defining the objective helps guide the entire survey design process.

2. **Identify the Target Population**: The target population refers to the group of individuals that the survey aims to represent. It is important to clearly define the characteristics and boundaries of the target population to ensure that the survey results are applicable to the intended audience.

3. **Choose the Survey Method**: There are various survey methods available, including face-to-face interviews, telephone surveys, online surveys, and paper-based surveys. Each method has its own advantages and limitations. Consider factors such as cost, time, and accessibility when choosing the most appropriate survey method for your study.

4. **Develop Survey Questions**: The survey questions should be clear, concise, and unbiased. Avoid leading or loaded questions that may influence respondents' answers. It is also important to consider the level of detail required in the responses and whether open-ended or closed-ended questions are more suitable.

5. **Pilot Test the Survey**: Before administering the survey to the target population, it is advisable to conduct a pilot test with a small group of individuals. This helps identify any potential issues or ambiguities in the survey questions and allows for necessary adjustments to be made.

6. **Administer the Survey**: Once the survey design is finalized, it is time to administer the survey to the target population. Ensure that the survey instructions are clear and provide any necessary guidance to respondents. Consider using random sampling techniques to select participants from the target population, if applicable.

7. **Analyze and Interpret the Data**: After collecting the survey responses, it is important to analyze and interpret the data. This may involve summarizing the data using descriptive statistics, conducting statistical tests, or identifying patterns and trends. The data analysis should be aligned with the research objective and research question.

8. **Draw Conclusions and Make Inferences**: Based on the analysis of the survey data, draw conclusions and make inferences about the target population. It is important to consider the limitations of the survey design and any potential sources of bias that may affect the validity of the conclusions.

Designing surveys requires careful planning and attention to detail. By following these steps, you can ensure that your survey provides valuable insights and contributes to the field of study design.

#### Next Steps

In the next section, we will explore the concept of sampling error and its impact on survey results. Understanding sampling error is crucial for interpreting survey data accurately and making informed decisions based on the findings. Let's dive into the world of sampling error in the next section!

### Section: Observational Studies and Experiments

In this section, we will explore the different types of study designs used in mathematics research. Study design refers to the overall plan or strategy that researchers use to investigate a particular topic or problem. By understanding the different study designs, you will be able to analyze and interpret mathematical data more effectively.

#### Designing Observational Studies

Observational studies are a type of study design where researchers observe and collect data on individuals or groups without intervening or manipulating any variables. These studies are often used when it is not feasible or ethical to conduct experiments. In observational studies, researchers simply observe and record data, allowing them to study relationships and patterns in a natural setting.

##### Types of Observational Studies

There are two main types of observational studies: cross-sectional studies and longitudinal studies.

**Cross-sectional Studies**: In a cross-sectional study, data is collected at a single point in time. Researchers gather information from a sample of individuals or groups and analyze the data to understand relationships or differences between variables. For example, a cross-sectional study may examine the relationship between students' math scores and their study habits by collecting data from different students at a specific time.

**Longitudinal Studies**: In a longitudinal study, data is collected over an extended period of time. Researchers follow a group of individuals or groups over time and collect data at multiple points. This allows them to study changes and trends over time. For example, a longitudinal study may track the math performance of a group of students from elementary school to high school to understand how their skills develop over time.

##### Steps in Designing Observational Studies

Designing an observational study involves careful planning and consideration of various factors. Here are some key steps to follow when designing an observational study:

1. **Define the Research Question**: The first step is to clearly define the research question or objective of the study. What specific information are you trying to gather? What relationship or pattern are you interested in studying? Defining the research question helps guide the entire study design process.

2. **Identify the Target Population**: The target population refers to the group of individuals or groups that the study aims to represent. It is important to clearly define the characteristics and boundaries of the target population to ensure that the study results are applicable to the intended audience.

3. **Choose the Sampling Method**: In observational studies, researchers often use sampling methods to select a representative sample from the target population. There are different sampling methods available, such as random sampling, stratified sampling, and cluster sampling. The choice of sampling method depends on factors such as the size of the target population and the resources available for data collection.

4. **Collect Data**: Once the sampling method is determined, researchers collect data by observing and recording information on the variables of interest. This can be done through surveys, interviews, or direct observations. It is important to ensure that the data collection process is standardized and consistent to maintain the reliability and validity of the data.

5. **Analyze and Interpret Data**: After collecting the data, researchers analyze and interpret the data to draw conclusions and make inferences about the target population. This involves using statistical techniques and mathematical models to analyze the relationships and patterns observed in the data.

By following these steps, researchers can design observational studies that provide valuable insights into mathematical phenomena and contribute to the field of mathematics research.

### Section: Observational Studies and Experiments

In this section, we will continue our exploration of study designs used in mathematics research. In the previous section, we discussed observational studies, which involve observing and collecting data on individuals or groups without intervening or manipulating any variables. Now, let's delve into the process of designing experiments, which is another type of study design commonly used in mathematics research.

#### Designing Experiments

Experiments are a powerful tool in mathematics research as they allow researchers to investigate cause-and-effect relationships between variables. In an experiment, researchers manipulate one or more variables and observe the effect on another variable. This allows them to draw conclusions about the relationship between the variables under study.

##### Steps in Designing Experiments

Designing an experiment involves careful planning and consideration of various factors. Here are some important steps to follow when designing an experiment:

1. **Identify the research question**: The first step in designing an experiment is to clearly define the research question or problem that you want to investigate. This will guide the entire experimental process and help you determine the variables to manipulate and measure.

2. **Formulate hypotheses**: Once you have identified the research question, you need to formulate hypotheses. A hypothesis is a statement that predicts the relationship between the variables under study. For example, if you are investigating the effect of studying time on math test scores, your hypothesis could be that increased studying time leads to higher test scores.

3. **Select the experimental design**: There are different types of experimental designs, each with its own advantages and limitations. The choice of experimental design depends on the research question and the resources available. Some common experimental designs include:

   - **Randomized controlled trials**: In a randomized controlled trial, participants are randomly assigned to different groups. One group receives the treatment or intervention being studied, while the other group serves as a control group. This design helps minimize bias and allows researchers to compare the effects of the treatment.

   - **Factorial design**: In a factorial design, researchers manipulate two or more independent variables to study their individual and combined effects on the dependent variable. This design allows for the investigation of interactions between variables.

   - **Quasi-experimental design**: In a quasi-experimental design, researchers do not have full control over the assignment of participants to groups. This design is often used when it is not feasible or ethical to randomly assign participants to groups.

4. **Determine the sample size**: The sample size refers to the number of participants or observations in the experiment. A larger sample size generally leads to more reliable results. Determining the appropriate sample size involves considering factors such as the desired level of statistical significance and the expected effect size.

5. **Collect and analyze data**: Once the experimental design is in place, you can proceed with data collection. This involves measuring the variables of interest and recording the data. After data collection, you can analyze the data using statistical methods to test your hypotheses and draw conclusions.

6. **Interpret and communicate results**: Finally, you need to interpret the results of your experiment and communicate them effectively. This involves analyzing the data, drawing conclusions, and discussing the implications of your findings. It is important to present your results in a clear and concise manner, using appropriate visualizations and statistical measures.

By following these steps, you can design and conduct experiments that provide valuable insights into mathematical phenomena and help advance our understanding of the subject.

In the next section, we will explore the process of data collection in observational studies and experiments. Stay tuned!

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the important topic of study design in integrated math. Study design is a crucial aspect of conducting research and collecting data, as it helps ensure the validity and reliability of the results. We have discussed various types of study designs, including observational studies and experimental studies, and the advantages and disadvantages of each. Additionally, we have examined the concept of sampling and different sampling techniques that can be used to select participants for a study. 

One key takeaway from this chapter is the importance of careful planning and consideration when designing a study. By selecting an appropriate study design and sampling technique, researchers can minimize bias and increase the generalizability of their findings. It is also crucial to consider ethical considerations and potential confounding variables that may impact the results. 

Overall, understanding study design is essential for anyone involved in research or data analysis. By mastering the concepts discussed in this chapter, readers will be equipped with the knowledge and skills necessary to design and conduct rigorous studies that yield meaningful and reliable results.

## Chapter: Rational Functions

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Rational Functions! In this chapter, we will explore the fascinating world of rational functions and delve into their properties, graphs, and applications. 

Rational functions are functions that can be expressed as the ratio of two polynomials. They are an essential part of the study of algebraic functions and play a significant role in various fields, including mathematics, physics, engineering, and economics.

Throughout this chapter, we will cover a wide range of topics related to rational functions. We will start by understanding the basic definition and notation of rational functions. We will then explore the domain and range of these functions and discuss any restrictions that may apply.

Next, we will examine the behavior of rational functions as the input approaches certain values, including vertical and horizontal asymptotes. We will also investigate the concept of holes in the graph of a rational function and learn how to identify and analyze them.

Furthermore, we will study the process of graphing rational functions and explore the effects of different parameters on the shape and behavior of the graph. We will discuss techniques such as finding intercepts, determining end behavior, and sketching the graph using transformations.

In addition to graphing, we will also explore the algebraic manipulation of rational functions. We will learn how to simplify, multiply, divide, and add rational expressions, as well as solve equations involving rational functions.

Finally, we will apply our knowledge of rational functions to real-world scenarios. We will examine various applications, such as modeling population growth, analyzing rates of change, and solving optimization problems.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of rational functions and be able to confidently analyze and graph them, as well as apply them to real-world situations. So let's dive in and master the world of rational functions!

### Section: Cancelling Common Factors

In this section, we will explore the concept of cancelling common factors in rational expressions. Simplifying rational expressions is an important skill that allows us to work with these expressions more easily and efficiently.

To begin, let's review what a rational expression is. A rational expression is a fraction in which the numerator and denominator are both polynomials. It can be written in the form:

$$
\frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}
$$

where $P(x)$ and $Q(x)$ are polynomials and $Q(x)$ is not equal to zero.

When simplifying rational expressions, we look for common factors in the numerator and denominator and cancel them out. This process is similar to simplifying fractions. By cancelling common factors, we can reduce the expression to its simplest form.

Let's look at an example to illustrate this concept:

Example 1:
Simplify the rational expression $\frac{2x^2 - 6x}{4x^2 - 12x}$.

Solution:
To simplify this expression, we first factor the numerator and denominator:

$$
\frac{2x(x - 3)}{4x(x - 3)}
$$

Notice that both the numerator and denominator have a common factor of $(x - 3)$. We can cancel out this common factor:

$$
\frac{2x}{4x}
$$

Simplifying further, we divide both the numerator and denominator by 2:

$$
\frac{x}{2}
$$

So, the simplified form of the rational expression is $\frac{x}{2}$.

It is important to note that when cancelling common factors, we must ensure that the denominator is not equal to zero after cancelling. If the denominator becomes zero, it would result in an undefined expression.

Example 2:
Simplify the rational expression $\frac{x^2 - 9}{x - 3}$.

Solution:
To simplify this expression, we first factor the numerator:

$$
\frac{(x + 3)(x - 3)}{x - 3}
$$

Notice that the numerator and denominator have a common factor of $(x - 3)$. We can cancel out this common factor:

$$
\frac{x + 3}{1}
$$

Simplifying further, we are left with $x + 3$.

In summary, when simplifying rational expressions, we look for common factors in the numerator and denominator and cancel them out. This process allows us to reduce the expression to its simplest form. However, we must be cautious and ensure that the denominator is not equal to zero after cancelling.

### Section: Cancelling Common Factors

In this section, we will continue our exploration of cancelling common factors in rational expressions. Simplifying rational expressions is an important skill that allows us to work with these expressions more easily and efficiently.

To recap, a rational expression is a fraction in which the numerator and denominator are both polynomials. It can be written in the form:

$$
\frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}
$$

where $P(x)$ and $Q(x)$ are polynomials and $Q(x)$ is not equal to zero.

When simplifying rational expressions, we look for common factors in the numerator and denominator and cancel them out. This process is similar to simplifying fractions. By cancelling common factors, we can reduce the expression to its simplest form.

Let's look at another example to illustrate this concept:

Example 3:
Simplify the rational expression $\frac{3x^3 - 9x^2}{6x^2 - 18x}$.

Solution:
To simplify this expression, we first factor the numerator and denominator:

$$
\frac{3x^2(x - 3)}{6x(x - 3)}
$$

Notice that both the numerator and denominator have a common factor of $(x - 3)$. We can cancel out this common factor:

$$
\frac{3x^2}{6x}
$$

Simplifying further, we divide both the numerator and denominator by 3:

$$
\frac{x^2}{2x}
$$

Finally, we simplify the expression by canceling out a common factor of $x$:

$$
\frac{x}{2}
$$

So, the simplified form of the rational expression is $\frac{x}{2}$.

It is important to note that when cancelling common factors, we must ensure that the denominator is not equal to zero after cancelling. If the denominator becomes zero, it would result in an undefined expression.

Now, let's explore some applications of cancelling common factors in rational expressions.

#### Applications of Cancelling Common Factors

Cancelling common factors in rational expressions can be useful in various real-world scenarios. Here are a few examples:

1. **Simplifying formulas**: Rational expressions often appear in mathematical formulas used in science, engineering, and other fields. By simplifying these expressions using cancelling common factors, we can make the formulas easier to work with and understand.

2. **Solving equations**: When solving equations involving rational expressions, cancelling common factors can help us simplify the expressions and make the equations more manageable. This can make it easier to find solutions to the equations.

3. **Graphing rational functions**: Rational functions are functions that can be expressed as a ratio of two polynomials. Cancelling common factors in the rational expressions can help us identify important features of the graph, such as vertical asymptotes and holes.

These are just a few examples of how cancelling common factors in rational expressions can be applied in real-world situations. As you continue to study rational functions, you will encounter more applications and gain a deeper understanding of their significance.

In the next section, we will explore another important concept related to rational functions: adding and subtracting rational expressions.

### Section: End Behavior of Rational Functions

In this section, we will explore the end behavior of rational functions. Understanding the end behavior of a function can provide valuable insights into its overall shape and behavior.

#### Definition of End Behavior

The end behavior of a function refers to what happens to the function's values as the input approaches positive or negative infinity. It helps us understand how the function behaves at the far ends of the x-axis.

For rational functions, which are functions in the form of $\frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}$, where $P(x)$ and $Q(x)$ are polynomials, the end behavior is determined by the degrees of the numerator and denominator.

Let's consider two cases:

1. If the degree of the numerator is greater than the degree of the denominator, the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the leading term of the numerator. 

   - If the leading term of the numerator is positive, the function will have a positive end behavior as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.
   
   - If the leading term of the numerator is negative, the function will have a negative end behavior as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.

2. If the degree of the denominator is greater than or equal to the degree of the numerator, the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the leading term of the denominator.

   - If the leading term of the denominator is positive, the function will have a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$ as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.
   
   - If the leading term of the denominator is negative, the function will have a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$ as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity, but the function will be reflected across the x-axis.

Let's look at an example to illustrate the concept of end behavior:

Example 1:
Consider the rational function $f(x) = \frac{2x^3 - 5x^2 + 3}{x^2 + 1}$.

To determine the end behavior of this function, we compare the degrees of the numerator and denominator. The degree of the numerator is 3, and the degree of the denominator is 2.

Since the degree of the numerator is greater than the degree of the denominator, the end behavior is determined by the leading term of the numerator, which is $2x^3$.

As $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity, the leading term $2x^3$ becomes infinitely large. Therefore, the end behavior of the function is that it approaches positive or negative infinity.

It is important to note that the end behavior of a rational function can be influenced by other factors such as the presence of vertical asymptotes or holes in the graph. These factors can alter the overall behavior of the function.

Understanding the end behavior of rational functions can help us analyze and interpret their graphs. It provides valuable information about how the function behaves at the far ends of the x-axis, allowing us to make predictions and draw conclusions about the function's overall shape and behavior.

In the next section, we will explore the graphing of rational functions and how to identify key features such as vertical asymptotes, horizontal asymptotes, and holes.

### Section: End Behavior of Rational Functions

In this section, we will continue our exploration of the end behavior of rational functions. Understanding the end behavior of a function can provide valuable insights into its overall shape and behavior.

#### Determining End Behavior from Rational Functions

To determine the end behavior of a rational function, we need to consider the degrees of the numerator and denominator. Let's review the two cases we discussed in the previous section:

1. If the degree of the numerator is greater than the degree of the denominator, the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the leading term of the numerator. 

   - If the leading term of the numerator is positive, the function will have a positive end behavior as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.
   
   - If the leading term of the numerator is negative, the function will have a negative end behavior as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.

2. If the degree of the denominator is greater than or equal to the degree of the numerator, the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the leading term of the denominator.

   - If the leading term of the denominator is positive, the function will have a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$ as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.
   
   - If the leading term of the denominator is negative, the function will have a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$ as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity, but the function will be reflected across the x-axis.

Let's continue with the example we started in the previous section to illustrate the concept of end behavior:

#### Example 1:
Consider the rational function $f(x) = \frac{2x^3 - 5x^2 + 3}{x^2 + 1}$.

To determine the end behavior of this function, we compare the degrees of the numerator and denominator. The degree of the numerator is 3, and the degree of the denominator is 2.

Since the degree of the numerator is greater than the degree of the denominator, the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the leading term of the numerator, which is $2x^3$.

Since the leading term of the numerator is positive, the function will have a positive end behavior as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.

Now that we have determined the end behavior of this function, we can use this information to better understand its overall shape and behavior.

In the next section, we will explore more examples to solidify our understanding of determining end behavior from rational functions.

### Section: Discontinuities of Rational Functions

In this section, we will explore the concept of discontinuities in rational functions. A discontinuity occurs when a function is not continuous at a certain point or interval. Understanding the types of discontinuities that can occur in rational functions is crucial in analyzing their behavior.

#### Types of Discontinuities

There are three main types of discontinuities that can occur in rational functions: removable, jump, and infinite discontinuities. Let's discuss each type in detail:

#### 1. Removable Discontinuity

A removable discontinuity, also known as a hole, occurs when a function has a point where it is undefined, but it can be made continuous by assigning a value to that point. This means that the function has a hole in its graph at that particular point.

To determine if a rational function has a removable discontinuity, we need to check if there is a common factor between the numerator and denominator that can be canceled out. If there is, then the function has a removable discontinuity at that point.

#### 2. Jump Discontinuity

A jump discontinuity occurs when the function has two distinct limits as it approaches a certain point. In other words, the function "jumps" from one value to another at that point. This results in a gap or jump in the graph of the function.

To identify a jump discontinuity in a rational function, we need to check if the function has different values as it approaches the point from the left and the right. If the function has different limits from each side, then it has a jump discontinuity at that point.

#### 3. Infinite Discontinuity

An infinite discontinuity occurs when the function approaches positive or negative infinity as it approaches a certain point. This means that the function has a vertical asymptote at that point, where the graph of the function becomes infinitely steep.

To determine if a rational function has an infinite discontinuity, we need to check if the denominator becomes zero at a certain point. If the denominator becomes zero and the numerator does not, then the function has an infinite discontinuity at that point.

Let's continue with the example we started in the previous section to illustrate the concept of discontinuities:

#### Example 2:
Consider the rational function $g(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x - 2}$.

To determine the discontinuities of this function, we need to check for any common factors, different limits, and points where the denominator becomes zero.

First, let's check for any common factors between the numerator and denominator. In this case, there are no common factors that can be canceled out, so there are no removable discontinuities.

Next, let's check for different limits as the function approaches a certain point. As $x$ approaches 2 from the left, the function approaches 0. However, as $x$ approaches 2 from the right, the function approaches positive infinity. Therefore, there is a jump discontinuity at $x = 2$.

Finally, let's check for points where the denominator becomes zero. In this case, the denominator becomes zero at $x = 2$. Since the numerator does not become zero at this point, the function has an infinite discontinuity at $x = 2$.

By understanding the types of discontinuities that can occur in rational functions, we can better analyze their behavior and make predictions about their graphs. In the next section, we will explore how to graph rational functions and identify their key features.

### Section: Discontinuities of Rational Functions

In this section, we will continue our exploration of discontinuities in rational functions. We have already discussed the three main types of discontinuities: removable, jump, and infinite discontinuities. Now, we will focus on how to identify these discontinuities from graphs.

#### Identifying Discontinuities from Graphs

When given the graph of a rational function, we can visually identify the presence of discontinuities. Let's take a closer look at each type of discontinuity and how it appears on a graph.

##### 1. Removable Discontinuity

A removable discontinuity, also known as a hole, is represented by an open circle on the graph. This indicates that the function is undefined at that point, but it can be made continuous by assigning a value to that point. The graph will have a gap or hole at the location of the open circle.

To determine if a rational function has a removable discontinuity from its graph, we need to check if there is a common factor between the numerator and denominator that can be canceled out. If there is, then the function has a removable discontinuity at that point.

##### 2. Jump Discontinuity

A jump discontinuity is represented by a vertical line or gap on the graph. This indicates that the function has two distinct limits as it approaches a certain point. In other words, the function "jumps" from one value to another at that point. The graph will have a visible gap or jump in its shape.

To identify a jump discontinuity in a rational function from its graph, we need to check if the function has different values as it approaches the point from the left and the right. If the function has different limits from each side, then it has a jump discontinuity at that point.

##### 3. Infinite Discontinuity

An infinite discontinuity is represented by a vertical asymptote on the graph. This indicates that the function approaches positive or negative infinity as it approaches a certain point. The graph will become infinitely steep near the vertical asymptote.

To determine if a rational function has an infinite discontinuity from its graph, we need to check if the function approaches positive or negative infinity as it approaches a certain point. If it does, then it has an infinite discontinuity at that point.

By analyzing the graph of a rational function, we can identify the presence of removable, jump, and infinite discontinuities. Understanding these discontinuities is crucial in analyzing the behavior of rational functions and their graphs.

In the next section, we will explore how to find the exact locations of these discontinuities and further analyze their impact on the function.

### Section: Graphs of Rational Functions

In this section, we will explore the graphs of rational functions. Rational functions are functions that can be expressed as the ratio of two polynomials. They are commonly written in the form:

$$
f(x) = \frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}
$$

where $P(x)$ and $Q(x)$ are polynomials and $Q(x)$ is not equal to zero.

#### Graphing Basic Rational Functions

To graph basic rational functions, we need to understand the key features that can help us visualize their behavior. These features include the x-intercepts, y-intercepts, vertical asymptotes, and horizontal asymptotes.

##### X-intercepts

The x-intercepts of a rational function are the points where the graph intersects the x-axis. To find the x-intercepts, we set the numerator of the rational function equal to zero and solve for x. The solutions to this equation will give us the x-coordinates of the x-intercepts.

##### Y-intercepts

The y-intercept of a rational function is the point where the graph intersects the y-axis. To find the y-intercept, we set x equal to zero in the rational function and evaluate the resulting expression. The value we obtain will give us the y-coordinate of the y-intercept.

##### Vertical Asymptotes

Vertical asymptotes are vertical lines that the graph of a rational function approaches but never touches. They occur when the denominator of the rational function is equal to zero, but the numerator is not. To find the vertical asymptotes, we set the denominator of the rational function equal to zero and solve for x. The solutions to this equation will give us the x-coordinates of the vertical asymptotes.

##### Horizontal Asymptotes

Horizontal asymptotes are horizontal lines that the graph of a rational function approaches as x approaches positive or negative infinity. The presence and location of horizontal asymptotes depend on the degrees of the numerator and denominator polynomials.

- If the degree of the numerator is less than the degree of the denominator, the horizontal asymptote is the x-axis (y = 0).
- If the degree of the numerator is equal to the degree of the denominator, the horizontal asymptote is the ratio of the leading coefficients of the numerator and denominator.
- If the degree of the numerator is greater than the degree of the denominator, there is no horizontal asymptote.

By understanding these key features, we can sketch the graph of a basic rational function. We start by plotting the x-intercepts and y-intercept, then draw the vertical asymptotes and horizontal asymptotes. Finally, we connect the points and curves to complete the graph.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the behavior of rational functions and explore more complex graphs.

### Section: Graphs of Rational Functions

In this section, we will continue our exploration of the graphs of rational functions. Rational functions are functions that can be expressed as the ratio of two polynomials. They are commonly written in the form:

$$
f(x) = \frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}
$$

where $P(x)$ and $Q(x)$ are polynomials and $Q(x)$ is not equal to zero.

#### Graphing Transformed Rational Functions

In the previous section, we learned about the key features of basic rational functions. Now, we will extend our understanding by exploring how to graph transformed rational functions. A transformed rational function is obtained by applying various transformations to the basic rational function.

##### Vertical and Horizontal Shifts

One common transformation is a vertical or horizontal shift of the graph. A vertical shift moves the graph up or down, while a horizontal shift moves the graph left or right. To perform a vertical shift, we add or subtract a constant value to the numerator or denominator of the rational function. This shifts the entire graph vertically. Similarly, to perform a horizontal shift, we add or subtract a constant value to the variable in the numerator or denominator. This shifts the graph horizontally.

##### Vertical and Horizontal Stretches/Compressions

Another transformation is a vertical or horizontal stretch or compression of the graph. A vertical stretch or compression changes the vertical scale of the graph, making it narrower or wider. To perform a vertical stretch or compression, we multiply the numerator or denominator by a constant value greater than 1 or between 0 and 1, respectively. This affects the vertical shape of the graph. Similarly, a horizontal stretch or compression changes the horizontal scale of the graph, making it narrower or wider. To perform a horizontal stretch or compression, we multiply the variable in the numerator or denominator by a constant value greater than 1 or between 0 and 1, respectively. This affects the horizontal shape of the graph.

##### Reflections

We can also reflect the graph of a rational function across the x-axis or y-axis. To reflect the graph across the x-axis, we multiply the entire rational function by -1. This flips the graph upside down. To reflect the graph across the y-axis, we multiply the numerator or denominator by -1. This flips the graph horizontally.

##### Combining Transformations

It is important to note that we can combine multiple transformations to obtain more complex graphs. For example, we can perform a vertical shift followed by a horizontal stretch, or a reflection followed by a horizontal shift. By understanding how each transformation affects the graph, we can accurately sketch the graph of a transformed rational function.

In the next section, we will explore specific examples of graphing transformed rational functions to solidify our understanding of these concepts.

### Section: Modeling with Rational Functions

In the previous section, we explored the graphs of rational functions and learned about their key features. Now, we will delve into the exciting world of modeling with rational functions. Rational functions are incredibly versatile and can be used to model a wide range of real-world phenomena.

#### The Power of Rational Function Models

Rational function models allow us to represent relationships between variables in a concise and meaningful way. They are particularly useful when dealing with situations that involve rates, proportions, or ratios. By using rational function models, we can gain insights into various scenarios and make predictions based on the patterns we observe.

#### Creating Rational Function Models

To create a rational function model, we need to identify the variables involved and determine how they are related. This often requires analyzing the problem at hand and understanding the context in which it occurs. Let's go through the steps involved in creating rational function models:

1. **Identify the variables:** Start by identifying the variables that are relevant to the problem. These variables represent the quantities that we want to model and understand.

2. **Establish the relationship:** Determine how the variables are related to each other. Are they directly proportional, inversely proportional, or is there another type of relationship? This step is crucial in setting up the correct form of the rational function.

3. **Write the rational function:** Once we have established the relationship, we can write the rational function that represents it. The rational function will have the form:

   $$
   f(x) = \frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}
   $$

   where $P(x)$ and $Q(x)$ are polynomials that depend on the variables involved. It's important to note that $Q(x)$ should not equal zero, as division by zero is undefined.

4. **Simplify the rational function:** Simplify the rational function by factoring out common factors and canceling out any common terms in the numerator and denominator. This step helps us obtain a more manageable and interpretable form of the model.

5. **Interpret the model:** Once we have the simplified rational function, we can interpret the model in the context of the problem. This involves understanding the meaning of the variables and the implications of the model's behavior.

6. **Validate and refine the model:** Finally, we need to validate the model by comparing its predictions to real-world data or known information. If the model doesn't match the observed behavior, we may need to refine it by considering additional factors or adjusting the parameters.

#### Example: Modeling Population Growth

Let's consider an example to illustrate the process of creating a rational function model. Suppose we want to model the population growth of a city over time. We know that the population is directly proportional to the number of years since a certain starting point.

1. **Identify the variables:** The variables in this scenario are the population (P) and the number of years (t).

2. **Establish the relationship:** Since the population is directly proportional to the number of years, we can write the relationship as:

   $$
   P = kt
   $$

   where k is a constant of proportionality.

3. **Write the rational function:** To create the rational function model, we rearrange the equation to solve for P:

   $$
   P = kt \implies f(t) = \frac{P}{t} = \frac{kt}{t} = k
   $$

   The rational function model simplifies to a constant value, k.

4. **Interpret the model:** In this case, the rational function model tells us that the population remains constant over time. This implies that the city's population does not change as the number of years increases.

5. **Validate and refine the model:** To validate the model, we can compare its predictions to actual population data for the city. If the model accurately represents the population growth, we can consider it valid. However, if the model doesn't match the observed data, we may need to refine it by considering additional factors or adjusting the constant of proportionality.

By following these steps, we can create rational function models that accurately represent real-world phenomena and help us gain a deeper understanding of the relationships between variables.

### Section: Modeling with Rational Functions

In the previous section, we explored the graphs of rational functions and learned about their key features. Now, let's delve into the exciting world of modeling with rational functions. Rational functions are incredibly versatile and can be used to model a wide range of real-world phenomena.

#### The Power of Rational Function Models

Rational function models allow us to represent relationships between variables in a concise and meaningful way. They are particularly useful when dealing with situations that involve rates, proportions, or ratios. By using rational function models, we can gain insights into various scenarios and make predictions based on the patterns we observe.

#### Creating Rational Function Models

To create a rational function model, we need to identify the variables involved and determine how they are related. This often requires analyzing the problem at hand and understanding the context in which it occurs. Let's go through the steps involved in creating rational function models:

1. **Identify the variables:** Start by identifying the variables that are relevant to the problem. These variables represent the quantities that we want to model and understand.

2. **Establish the relationship:** Determine how the variables are related to each other. Are they directly proportional, inversely proportional, or is there another type of relationship? This step is crucial in setting up the correct form of the rational function.

3. **Write the rational function:** Once we have established the relationship, we can write the rational function that represents it. The rational function will have the form:

   $$
   f(x) = \frac{P(x)}{Q(x)}
   $$

   where $P(x)$ and $Q(x)$ are polynomials that depend on the variables involved. It's important to note that $Q(x)$ should not equal zero, as division by zero is undefined.

4. **Simplify the rational function:** Simplify the rational function by factoring out common factors and canceling out any common terms in the numerator and denominator. This step helps us to simplify the expression and make it easier to work with.

Now that we have gone through the steps involved in creating rational function models, let's move on to solving rational function models in the next subsection.

### Section: Multiplying & Dividing Rational Expressions

In the previous section, we explored how to model real-world phenomena using rational functions. Now, let's dive into the world of multiplying and dividing rational expressions. This skill is essential for simplifying complex rational expressions and solving equations involving rational functions.

#### Rules for Multiplying Rational Expressions

When multiplying rational expressions, we can follow these rules:

1. **Multiply the numerators:** Multiply the numerators of the rational expressions together. This is done by multiplying the polynomials that represent the numerators.

2. **Multiply the denominators:** Multiply the denominators of the rational expressions together. Similarly, this is done by multiplying the polynomials that represent the denominators.

3. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out. This simplifies the expression and makes it easier to work with.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these rules:

Example 1:
Simplify the expression $\frac{2x}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{x^2}$.

Solution:
To simplify this expression, we follow the rules for multiplying rational expressions:

1. Multiply the numerators: $2x \cdot 4 = 8x$.

2. Multiply the denominators: $3 \cdot x^2 = 3x^2$.

3. Simplify if possible: In this case, there are no common factors between the numerator and denominator, so the expression cannot be further simplified.

Therefore, the simplified expression is $\frac{8x}{3x^2}$.

It's important to note that when multiplying rational expressions, we should always simplify the result if possible. This helps us avoid unnecessary complexity and ensures that our solutions are in the simplest form.

#### Rules for Dividing Rational Expressions

When dividing rational expressions, we can follow these rules:

1. **Keep the first rational expression:** Keep the first rational expression as it is.

2. **Multiply by the reciprocal:** Multiply the first rational expression by the reciprocal of the second rational expression. The reciprocal is obtained by swapping the numerator and denominator of the second rational expression.

3. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out. This simplifies the expression.

Let's look at an example to understand these rules:

Example 2:
Simplify the expression $\frac{3x}{4} \div \frac{2}{x^2}$.

Solution:
To simplify this expression, we follow the rules for dividing rational expressions:

1. Keep the first rational expression: $\frac{3x}{4}$.

2. Multiply by the reciprocal: $\frac{3x}{4} \cdot \frac{x^2}{2} = \frac{3x^3}{8}$.

3. Simplify if possible: In this case, there are no common factors between the numerator and denominator, so the expression cannot be further simplified.

Therefore, the simplified expression is $\frac{3x^3}{8}$.

By understanding and applying these rules, we can simplify complex rational expressions and solve equations involving rational functions. Practice these rules with various examples to strengthen your understanding and mastery of multiplying and dividing rational expressions.

### Section: Multiplying & Dividing Rational Expressions

In the previous section, we explored how to model real-world phenomena using rational functions. Now, let's dive deeper into the world of multiplying and dividing rational expressions. This skill is essential for simplifying complex rational expressions and solving equations involving rational functions.

#### Rules for Multiplying Rational Expressions

When multiplying rational expressions, we can follow these rules:

1. **Multiply the numerators:** Multiply the numerators of the rational expressions together. This is done by multiplying the polynomials that represent the numerators.

2. **Multiply the denominators:** Multiply the denominators of the rational expressions together. Similarly, this is done by multiplying the polynomials that represent the denominators.

3. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out. This simplifies the expression and makes it easier to work with.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these rules:

Example 1:
Simplify the expression $\frac{2x}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{x^2}$.

Solution:
To simplify this expression, we follow the rules for multiplying rational expressions:

1. Multiply the numerators: $2x \cdot 4 = 8x$.

2. Multiply the denominators: $3 \cdot x^2 = 3x^2$.

3. Simplify if possible: In this case, there are no common factors between the numerator and denominator, so the expression cannot be further simplified.

Therefore, the simplified expression is $\frac{8x}{3x^2}$.

It's important to note that when multiplying rational expressions, we should always simplify the result if possible. This helps us avoid unnecessary complexity and ensures that our solutions are in the simplest form.

#### Rules for Dividing Rational Expressions

When dividing rational expressions, we can follow these rules:

1. **Keep the first rational expression:** Keep the first rational expression as it is.

2. **Multiply by the reciprocal:** To divide by a rational expression, we multiply by its reciprocal. The reciprocal of a rational expression is obtained by swapping the numerator and denominator.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these rules:

Example 2:
Divide the expression $\frac{3x}{2} \div \frac{4}{x^2}$.

Solution:
To divide this expression, we follow the rules for dividing rational expressions:

1. Keep the first rational expression: $\frac{3x}{2}$.

2. Multiply by the reciprocal: To divide by $\frac{4}{x^2}$, we multiply by its reciprocal, which is $\frac{x^2}{4}$. 

   $\frac{3x}{2} \div \frac{4}{x^2} = \frac{3x}{2} \cdot \frac{x^2}{4}$.

3. Simplify if possible: In this case, we can simplify the expression further by canceling out common factors. The factor of 2 in the numerator cancels out with the factor of 2 in the denominator.

   $\frac{3x}{2} \cdot \frac{x^2}{4} = \frac{3x \cdot x^2}{2 \cdot 4} = \frac{3x^3}{8}$.

Therefore, the simplified expression is $\frac{3x^3}{8}$.

By following these rules, we can effectively divide rational expressions and simplify the results. This is crucial for solving equations involving rational functions and understanding the behavior of these functions.

### Section: Adding Subtracting Rational Expressions Intro

In the previous section, we learned about multiplying and dividing rational expressions. Now, let's move on to another important skill in working with rational functions: adding and subtracting rational expressions. 

When we add or subtract rational expressions, we are essentially combining them into a single expression. This is similar to adding or subtracting fractions, where we need to find a common denominator before performing the operation. 

To add or subtract rational expressions, we follow these steps:

1. **Find a common denominator:** Look for the least common multiple (LCM) of the denominators of the rational expressions. This will be our common denominator.

2. **Adjust the numerators:** Multiply the numerator and denominator of each rational expression by the necessary factors to make the denominators equal to the common denominator.

3. **Combine the numerators:** Once the denominators are the same, we can add or subtract the numerators together. 

4. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out to simplify the expression.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these steps:

Example 1:
Add the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$.

Solution:
To add these rational expressions, we follow the steps outlined above:

1. Find a common denominator: The denominators are $x+1$ and $x-2$. The LCM of these two expressions is $(x+1)(x-2)$.

2. Adjust the numerators: Multiply the first rational expression by $\frac{x-2}{x-2}$ and the second rational expression by $\frac{x+1}{x+1}$. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$ and $\frac{3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

3. Combine the numerators: Now that the denominators are the same, we can add the numerators together. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2) + 3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

4. Simplify if possible: We can simplify the numerator by distributing and combining like terms. This gives us $\frac{2x-4 + 3x+3}{(x+1)(x-2)}$. Simplifying further, we get $\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

Therefore, the sum of the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$ is $\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

It's important to note that when adding or subtracting rational expressions, we should always simplify the result if possible. This helps us avoid unnecessary complexity and ensures that our solutions are in the simplest form.

Now that we understand the steps for adding and subtracting rational expressions, let's move on to exploring some specific rules and examples in the next subsection.

### Section: Adding Subtracting Rational Expressions Intro

In the previous section, we learned about multiplying and dividing rational expressions. Now, let's move on to another important skill in working with rational functions: adding and subtracting rational expressions. 

When we add or subtract rational expressions, we are essentially combining them into a single expression. This is similar to adding or subtracting fractions, where we need to find a common denominator before performing the operation. 

To add or subtract rational expressions, we follow these steps:

1. **Find a common denominator:** Look for the least common multiple (LCM) of the denominators of the rational expressions. This will be our common denominator.

2. **Adjust the numerators:** Multiply the numerator and denominator of each rational expression by the necessary factors to make the denominators equal to the common denominator.

3. **Combine the numerators:** Once the denominators are the same, we can add or subtract the numerators together. 

4. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out to simplify the expression.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these steps:

Example 1:
Add the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$.

Solution:
To add these rational expressions, we follow the steps outlined above:

1. Find a common denominator: The denominators are $x+1$ and $x-2$. The LCM of these two expressions is $(x+1)(x-2)$.

2. Adjust the numerators: Multiply the first rational expression by $\frac{x-2}{x-2}$ and the second rational expression by $\frac{x+1}{x+1}$. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$ and $\frac{3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

3. Combine the numerators: Now that the denominators are the same, we can add the numerators together. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2) + 3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

4. Simplify if possible: We can simplify the numerator by distributing and combining like terms. This gives us $\frac{2x-4 + 3x+3}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

Now, let's simplify the numerator further by combining like terms: $\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

Therefore, the sum of the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$ is $\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

In the next section, we will explore more examples and practice problems to solidify our understanding of adding and subtracting rational expressions.

### Section: Adding Subtracting Rational Expressions (Factored)

In the previous section, we learned about adding and subtracting rational expressions with like denominators. Now, let's move on to a more challenging scenario: adding and subtracting rational expressions with different denominators. 

When the denominators of the rational expressions are different, we need to find a common denominator before we can add or subtract them. In this section, we will focus on adding and subtracting factored rational expressions. 

To add or subtract factored rational expressions, we follow these steps:

1. **Factor the denominators:** Before finding a common denominator, we need to factor the denominators of the rational expressions. This will help us identify the common factors and determine the least common multiple (LCM) of the denominators.

2. **Find a common denominator:** Look for the LCM of the factored denominators. This will be our common denominator.

3. **Adjust the numerators:** Multiply the numerator and denominator of each rational expression by the necessary factors to make the denominators equal to the common denominator.

4. **Combine the numerators:** Once the denominators are the same, we can add or subtract the numerators together. 

5. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out to simplify the expression.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these steps:

Example 1:
Add the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$.

Solution:
To add these rational expressions, we follow the steps outlined above:

1. Factor the denominators: The denominators are $x+1$ and $x-2$, which are already factored.

2. Find a common denominator: The LCM of $(x+1)$ and $(x-2)$ is $(x+1)(x-2)$.

3. Adjust the numerators: Multiply the first rational expression by $\frac{x-2}{x-2}$ and the second rational expression by $\frac{x+1}{x+1}$. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$ and $\frac{3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

4. Combine the numerators: Now that the denominators are the same, we can add the numerators together. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2) + 3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

5. Simplify if possible: We can simplify the numerator by distributing and combining like terms. This gives us $\frac{2x-4 + 3x+3}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

Now, let's simplify the numerator further:

$\frac{2x-4 + 3x+3}{(x+1)(x-2)}$

$=\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$

So, the sum of $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$ is $\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

In the next section, we will explore more examples and practice problems to solidify our understanding of adding and subtracting factored rational expressions.

### Section: Adding and Subtracting Rational Expressions with Unlike Denominators

In the previous section, we learned about adding and subtracting rational expressions with like denominators. Now, let's move on to a more challenging scenario: adding and subtracting rational expressions with different denominators. 

When the denominators of the rational expressions are different, we need to find a common denominator before we can add or subtract them. In this section, we will focus on adding and subtracting factored rational expressions. 

To add or subtract factored rational expressions, we follow these steps:

1. **Factor the denominators:** Before finding a common denominator, we need to factor the denominators of the rational expressions. This will help us identify the common factors and determine the least common multiple (LCM) of the denominators.

2. **Find a common denominator:** Look for the LCM of the factored denominators. This will be our common denominator.

3. **Adjust the numerators:** Multiply the numerator and denominator of each rational expression by the necessary factors to make the denominators equal to the common denominator.

4. **Combine the numerators:** Once the denominators are the same, we can add or subtract the numerators together. 

5. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out to simplify the expression.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these steps:

Example 1:
Add the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$.

Solution:
To add these rational expressions, we follow the steps outlined above:

1. Factor the denominators: The denominators are $x+1$ and $x-2$, which are already factored.

2. Find a common denominator: The LCM of $(x+1)$ and $(x-2)$ is $(x+1)(x-2)$.

3. Adjust the numerators: Multiply the first rational expression by $\frac{x-2}{x-2}$ and the second rational expression by $\frac{x+1}{x+1}$. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$ and $\frac{3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

4. Combine the numerators: Now that the denominators are the same, we can add the numerators together. $\frac{2(x-2)}{(x+1)(x-2)} + \frac{3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)} = \frac{2(x-2) + 3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

5. Simplify if possible: We can simplify the numerator by distributing and combining like terms. $\frac{2x-4 + 3x+3}{(x+1)(x-2)} = \frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

Therefore, the sum of the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$ is $\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

Now that we have learned how to add and subtract rational expressions with unlike denominators, we can apply these steps to solve more complex problems. Remember to always simplify your final answer if possible.

### Section: Adding Subtracting Rational Expressions (Not Factored)

In the previous section, we learned about adding and subtracting rational expressions with unlike denominators. Now, let's take it a step further and explore how to simplify these expressions by factoring.

#### Factoring to Simplify Addition and Subtraction

When adding or subtracting rational expressions with unlike denominators, it can be helpful to factor the expressions before finding a common denominator. Factoring allows us to identify any common factors and simplify the expressions further.

To factor a rational expression, we follow these steps:

1. **Factor the numerators and denominators:** Break down the expressions into their prime factors. This will help us identify any common factors.

2. **Identify common factors:** Look for any factors that appear in both the numerator and denominator. These common factors can be canceled out to simplify the expression.

3. **Find a common denominator:** Once we have factored the expressions, we can find a common denominator by multiplying the factored denominators together.

4. **Adjust the numerators:** Multiply the numerators by the necessary factors to make the denominators equal to the common denominator.

5. **Combine the numerators:** Once the denominators are the same, we can add or subtract the numerators together.

6. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out to simplify the expression further.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these steps:

Example 1:
Add the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$.

Solution:
To add these rational expressions, we follow the steps outlined above:

1. Factor the numerators and denominators: The numerators are already factored, but the denominators are $x+1$ and $x-2$, which are not factored.

2. Identify common factors: There are no common factors between the numerators and denominators.

3. Find a common denominator: The common denominator is $(x+1)(x-2)$.

4. Adjust the numerators: Multiply the first rational expression by $\frac{x-2}{x-2}$ and the second rational expression by $\frac{x+1}{x+1}$. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$ and $\frac{3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

5. Combine the numerators: Now that the denominators are the same, we can add the numerators together. This gives us $\frac{2(x-2) + 3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

6. Simplify if possible: There are no common factors between the numerator and denominator, so the expression $\frac{2(x-2) + 3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$ is already simplified.

By following these steps, we can simplify and add or subtract rational expressions with unlike denominators. Factoring the expressions allows us to identify common factors and simplify the expressions further.

### Section: Adding Subtracting Rational Expressions (Not Factored)

In the previous section, we learned about adding and subtracting rational expressions with unlike denominators. Now, let's take it a step further and explore how to simplify these expressions by factoring.

#### Factoring to Simplify Addition and Subtraction

When adding or subtracting rational expressions with unlike denominators, it can be helpful to factor the expressions before finding a common denominator. Factoring allows us to identify any common factors and simplify the expressions further.

To factor a rational expression, we follow these steps:

1. **Factor the numerators and denominators:** Break down the expressions into their prime factors. This will help us identify any common factors.

2. **Identify common factors:** Look for any factors that appear in both the numerator and denominator. These common factors can be canceled out to simplify the expression.

3. **Find a common denominator:** Once we have factored the expressions, we can find a common denominator by multiplying the factored denominators together.

4. **Adjust the numerators:** Multiply the numerators by the necessary factors to make the denominators equal to the common denominator.

5. **Combine the numerators:** Once the denominators are the same, we can add or subtract the numerators together.

6. **Simplify if possible:** If there are any common factors between the numerator and denominator, cancel them out to simplify the expression further.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these steps:

Example 1:
Add the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$.

Solution:
To add these rational expressions, we follow the steps outlined above:

1. Factor the numerators and denominators: The numerators are already factored, but the denominators are $x+1$ and $x-2$, which are not factored.

2. Identify common factors: There are no common factors between the numerators and denominators.

3. Find a common denominator: To find a common denominator, we multiply the factored denominators together: $(x+1)(x-2)$.

4. Adjust the numerators: We multiply the numerators by the necessary factors to make the denominators equal to the common denominator. In this case, we multiply the numerator of the first expression by $(x-2)$ and the numerator of the second expression by $(x+1)$.

5. Combine the numerators: Now that the denominators are the same, we can add the numerators together. The new numerator is $2(x-2) + 3(x+1)$.

6. Simplify if possible: We can simplify the numerator further by distributing and combining like terms. The simplified numerator is $2x - 4 + 3x + 3$.

7. Combine like terms: We combine like terms in the numerator to get $5x - 1$.

8. Final answer: The sum of the rational expressions $\frac{2}{x+1}$ and $\frac{3}{x-2}$ is $\frac{5x - 1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

By following these steps, we can simplify and add or subtract rational expressions with unlike denominators. This process allows us to work with these expressions more easily and solve various mathematical problems.

#### Subsection: Applications of Adding and Subtracting Rational Expressions

Now that we have learned how to add and subtract rational expressions, let's explore some applications of this concept. Adding and subtracting rational expressions can be useful in various real-world scenarios, such as:

1. **Mixing Solutions:** In chemistry, when two different solutions are mixed together, the resulting solution can be represented as a rational expression. By adding or subtracting the rational expressions that represent the individual solutions, we can determine the properties of the resulting mixture.

2. **Rate Problems:** When dealing with rates, such as speed or flow rates, rational expressions can be used to represent the relationship between different variables. By adding or subtracting these rational expressions, we can analyze the combined effect of multiple rates.

3. **Finance:** Rational expressions can also be used in financial calculations. For example, when calculating the total cost of a loan with different interest rates or payment schedules, adding or subtracting rational expressions can help determine the overall cost.

These are just a few examples of how adding and subtracting rational expressions can be applied in real-world situations. By mastering this concept, you will be equipped to solve a wide range of problems in various fields.

In the next section, we will explore another aspect of rational functions: multiplying and dividing rational expressions.

## Chapter: Rational Functions

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Rational Functions! In this chapter, we will explore the fascinating world of rational functions and delve into their properties, graphs, and applications. 

Rational functions are functions that can be expressed as the quotient of two polynomials. They are an essential topic in Integrated Math 3 as they provide a deeper understanding of algebraic expressions and equations. By studying rational functions, we can gain insights into various mathematical concepts such as asymptotes, domain and range, and transformations.

Throughout this chapter, we will cover a wide range of topics related to rational functions. We will start by examining the basic structure of rational functions and understanding how to simplify and evaluate them. We will then move on to exploring the behavior of rational functions by analyzing their graphs and identifying key features such as vertical and horizontal asymptotes.

Furthermore, we will investigate the concept of domain and range for rational functions and learn how to determine the restrictions on the variables. We will also explore the process of solving equations involving rational functions and discuss real-life applications where rational functions play a crucial role.

By the end of this chapter, you will have mastered the fundamental concepts and techniques related to rational functions. You will be able to confidently analyze and graph rational functions, solve equations involving them, and apply your knowledge to real-world scenarios.

So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of rational functions and unlock the mysteries they hold!

### Section: Adding Subtracting Rational Expressions (Not Factored)

In this section, we will explore the process of adding and subtracting rational expressions that are not factored. This skill is essential in solving various mathematical problems involving rational functions.

To begin, let's review what a rational expression is. A rational expression is a fraction in which the numerator and denominator are polynomials. For example, $\frac{3x}{x^2 + 2x - 3}$ is a rational expression.

When adding or subtracting rational expressions, we need to find a common denominator. The common denominator is the least common multiple (LCM) of the denominators of the rational expressions. Once we have a common denominator, we can combine the numerators and simplify the resulting expression.

Let's look at an example to illustrate this process:

Example 1:
Simplify the expression $\frac{2}{x+1} + \frac{3}{x-2}$.

To find a common denominator, we need to factor the denominators. The denominator $x+1$ cannot be factored further, but the denominator $x-2$ can be factored as $(x-2)$. Therefore, the common denominator is $(x+1)(x-2)$.

Next, we need to rewrite each fraction with the common denominator:

$\frac{2}{x+1} + \frac{3}{x-2} = \frac{2(x-2)}{(x+1)(x-2)} + \frac{3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$

Now, we can combine the numerators:

$\frac{2(x-2) + 3(x+1)}{(x+1)(x-2)}$

Simplifying the numerator:

$\frac{2x-4 + 3x+3}{(x+1)(x-2)}$

Combining like terms:

$\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$

And there you have it! The simplified expression is $\frac{5x-1}{(x+1)(x-2)}$.

#### Subsection: Applications of Adding and Subtracting Rational Expressions

In this subsection, we will explore some real-life applications where adding and subtracting rational expressions can be useful.

One common application is in the field of finance. When dealing with loans or investments, it is often necessary to calculate the total value or interest accrued over time. By representing the loan or investment as a rational expression, we can use the skills of adding and subtracting rational expressions to simplify and analyze the financial situation.

For example, let's say you have two loans with different interest rates. Loan A has an interest rate of 5% and Loan B has an interest rate of 7%. You want to calculate the total amount owed after a certain period of time. By representing the amount owed for each loan as a rational expression, you can add them together to find the total amount owed.

Another application is in physics, specifically when dealing with motion problems. In some cases, the motion of an object can be described by rational expressions. By adding or subtracting these rational expressions, we can analyze the overall motion of the object and make predictions about its behavior.

For instance, consider a car traveling at a constant speed of 60 miles per hour. The distance traveled by the car can be represented by a rational expression. If we want to calculate the total distance traveled after a certain amount of time, we can add the rational expressions that represent the distance traveled in each time interval.

These are just a few examples of how adding and subtracting rational expressions can be applied in real-life situations. By mastering this skill, you will be equipped to solve a wide range of problems and make connections between mathematics and the world around you.
